10068-8{_}2236{_}CHAPTER XI  THE NEW BOARDER  Pap Himes was sitting on the front gallery, dozing in the westering sunshine.
10068-8{_}2237{_}On his lap the big, yellow cat purred and blinked with a grotesque resemblance in colouring and expression to his master.
10068-8{_}2238{_}It was Sunday afternoon, when the toilers were all out of the mills, and most of them lying on their beds or gone in to Watauga.
10068-8{_}2239{_}The village seemed curiously silent and deserted.
10068-8{_}2240{_}Through the lazy smoke from his cob pipe Pap noticed Shade Buckheath emerge from the store and start up the street.
10068-8{_}2241{_}He paid no more attention till the young man's voice at the porch edge roused him from his half-somnolence.
10068-8{_}2242{_}"Evenin', Pap," said the newcomer.
10068-8{_}2243{_}"Good evenin' yourself," returned Himes with unusual cordiality.
10068-8{_}2244{_}He liked men, particularly young, vigorous, masterful men.
10068-8{_}2245{_}"Come in, Buck, an' set a spell.
10068-8{_}2246{_}Rest your hat--rest your hat."
10068-8{_}2247{_}It was always Pap's custom to call Shade by the first syllable of his second name.
10068-8{_}2248{_}Buck is a common by-name for boys in the mountains, and it could not be guessed whether the old man used it as a diminutive of the surname, or whether he meant merely to nickname this favourite of his.
10068-8{_}2249{_}Shade threw himself on the upper step of the porch and searched in his pockets for tobacco.
10068-8{_}2250{_}"Room for another boarder?"
10068-8{_}2251{_}he asked laconically.
10068-8{_}2252{_}The old man nodded.
10068-8{_}2253{_}"I reckon there's always room, ef it's asked for," he returned.
10068-8{_}2254{_}"Hit's the one way I got to make me a livin', with Louvany dyin' off and Mavity puny like she is.
10068-8{_}2255{_}I have obliged to keep the house full, or we'd see the bottom of the meal sack."
10068-8{_}2256{_}"All right," agreed Buckheath, rising, and treating the matter as terminated.
10068-8{_}2257{_}"I'll move my things in a-Monday."
10068-8{_}2258{_}"Hold on thar--hold on, young feller," objected Pap, as Shade turned away.
10068-8{_}2259{_}It was against all reasonable mountain precedent to trade so quickly; but indeed Shade had merely done so with a view to forcing through what he well knew to be a doubtful proposition.
10068-8{_}2260{_}"I'm a-holding on," he observed gruffly at last, as the other continued to blink at him with red eyes and say nothing.
10068-8{_}2261{_}"What's the matter with what I said?
10068-8{_}2262{_}You told me you had room for another boarder and I named it that I was comin' to board at your house.
10068-8{_}2263{_}Have you got any objections?"
10068-8{_}2264{_}"Well, yes, I have," Himes opened up ponderously.
10068-8{_}2265{_}"You set yourself down on that thar step and we'll have this here thing out.
10068-8{_}2266{_}My boardin'-house is for gals.
10068-8{_}2267{_}I fixed it so when I come here.
10068-8{_}2268{_}There ain't scarcely a rowdy feller in Cottonville that hain't at one time or another had the notion he'd board with Pap Himes; but I've always kep' a respectable house, and I always aim to, I am a old man, and I bear a good name, and I'm the only man in this house, and I aim to stay so.
10068-8{_}2269{_}Now, sir, there's my flatform; and you may take it or leave it."
10068-8{_}2270{_}Buckheath glanced angrily and contemptuously into the stupid, fatuous countenance above him; he appeared to curb with some difficulty the disposition to retort in kind.
10068-8{_}2271{_}Instead, he returned, sarcastically:  "The fellers around town say you won't keep anything but gals because nothin' but gals would put up with your hectorin' 'em, and crowdin' ten in a room that was intended for four.
10068-8{_}2272{_}That's what folks say; but I've got a reason to want to board with you, Pap, and I'll pay regular prices and take what you give me."
10068-8{_}2273{_}Himes looked a little astonished; then an expression of distrust stole over his broad, flat face.
10068-8{_}2274{_}"What's bringin' you here?"
10068-8{_}2275{_}he asked bluntly.
10068-8{_}2276{_}"Johnnie Consadine," returned Shade, without evasion or preamble.
10068-8{_}2277{_}"Before I left the mountains, Johnnie an' me was aimin' to wed. Now she's got down here, and doin' better than ever she hoped to, and I cain't get within hand-reach of her."
10068-8{_}2278{_}"Ye cain't?"
10068-8{_}2279{_}inquired Pap scornfully.
10068-8{_}2280{_}"Why anybody could marry that gal that wanted to.
10068-8{_}2281{_}But Lord!
10068-8{_}2282{_}anybody can marry _any_ gal, if he's got the sense he was born with."
10068-8{_}2283{_}"All right," repeated Shade grimly.
10068-8{_}2284{_}"I come to you to know could I get board, not to ask advice.
10068-8{_}2285{_}I aim to marry Johnnie Consadine, and I know my own business--air you goin' to board me?"
10068-8{_}2286{_}The old man turned this speech in his mind for some time.
10068-8{_}2287{_}"Curious," he muttered to himself, "how these here young fellers will get petted on some special gal and break their necks to have her."
10068-8{_}2288{_}"Shut up--will you?"
10068-8{_}2289{_}ejaculated Buckheath, so suddenly and fiercely that the old man fairly jumped, rousing the yellow cat to remonstrative squirmings.
10068-8{_}2290{_}"I tell you I know my business, and I ask no advice of you--will you board me?"
10068-8{_}2291{_}"I cain't do it, Buck," returned Himes definitely.
10068-8{_}2292{_}"I ain't got such a room to give you by yourself as you'd be willin' to take up with; and nobody comes into my room.
10068-8{_}2293{_}But I'll tell you what I'll do for you--I'll meal you, ef that will help your case any.
10068-8{_}2294{_}I'll meal you for two dollars a week, and throw in a good word with Johnnie."
10068-8{_}2295{_}Buckheath received the conclusion of this speech with a grin.
10068-8{_}2296{_}"I reckon your good word 'd have a lot to do with Johnnie Consadine," he said ironically, as he picked up his hat from the floor.
10068-8{_}2297{_}"Uh-huh," nodded Pap.
10068-8{_}2298{_}"She sets a heap of store by what I say.
10068-8{_}2299{_}All of 'em does; but Johnnie in particular.
10068-8{_}2300{_}I don't know but what you're about right.
10068-8{_}2301{_}Ain't no sense in bein' all tore up concernin' any gal or woman; but I believe if I was pickin' out a good worker that would earn her way, I'd as soon pick out Johnnie Consadine as any of 'em."
10068-8{_}2302{_}And having thus paid his ultimate compliment to Johnnie, Himes relapsed into intermittent slumber as Shade moved away down the squalid, dusty street under the fierce July sun.
10068-8{_}2303{_}Johnnie greeted the new boarder with a reserve which was in marked contrast to the reception he got from the other girls.
10068-8{_}2304{_}Shade Buckheath was a handsome, compelling fellow, and a good match; this Adamless Eden regarded him as a rival in glory even to Pap himself.
10068-8{_}2305{_}When supper was over on the first night of his arrival, Shade walked out on the porch and seated himself on the steps.
10068-8{_}2306{_}The girls disposed themselves at a little distance--your mountain-bred young female is ever obviously shy, almost to prudery.
10068-8{_}2307{_}"Whar's Johnnie Consadine?"
10068-8{_}2308{_}asked the newcomer lazily, disposing himself with his back against a post and his long legs stretched across the upper step.
10068-8{_}2309{_}"Settin' in thar, readin' a book," replied Beulah Catlett curtly.
10068-8{_}2310{_}Beulah was but fourteen, and she belonged to the newer dispensation which speaks up more boldly to the masculine half of creation.
10068-8{_}2311{_}"Johnnie!
10068-8{_}2312{_}Johnnie Consadine!"
10068-8{_}2313{_}she called through the casement.
10068-8{_}2314{_}"Here's Mr. Buckheath, wishful of your company.
10068-8{_}2315{_}Better come out."
10068-8{_}2316{_}"I will, after a while," returned Johnnie absently.
10068-8{_}2317{_}"I've got to help Aunt Mavity some, and then I'll be there."
10068-8{_}2318{_}"Hit's a sight, the books that gal does read," complained Beulah.
10068-8{_}2319{_}"Looks like a body might get enough stayin' in the house by workin' in a cotton mill, without humpin' theirselves up over a book all evenin'."
10068-8{_}2320{_}"Mr. Stoddard lends 'em to her," announced Mandy importantly.
10068-8{_}2321{_}"He used to give 'em to Miss Lyddy Sessions, and she'd give 'em to Johnnie; but now when Miss Lyddy's away, he'll bring one down to the mill about every so often, and him an' Johnnie'll stand and gas and talk over what's in 'em--I cain't understand one word they say.
10068-8{_}2322{_}I tell you Johnnie Consadine's got sense."
10068-8{_}2323{_}Her pride in Johnnie made her miss the look of rage that settled on Buckheath's face at her announcement.
10068-8{_}2324{_}The young fellow was glad when Pap Himes began to speak growlingly.
10068-8{_}2325{_}"Yes, an' if she was my gal I'd talk to her with a hickory about that there business.
10068-8{_}2326{_}A gal that ain't too old to carry on that-a-way ain't too old to take a whippin' for it.
10068-8{_}2327{_}Huh!"
10068-8{_}2328{_}For her own self Mandy would have been thoroughly scared by this attack; in Johnnie's defence she rustled her feathers like an old hen whose one chick has been menaced.
10068-8{_}2329{_}"Johnnie Consadine is the prettiest-behaved gal I ever seen," she announced shrilly.
10068-8{_}2330{_}"She ain't never said nor done the least thing that she hadn't ort.
10068-8{_}2331{_}Mr. Stoddard he just sees how awful smart she is, and he loves to lend her books and talk with her about 'em afterward.
10068-8{_}2332{_}For my part I ain't never seen look nor motion about Mr. Gray Stoddard that wasn't such as a gentleman ort to be.
10068-8{_}2333{_}I know he never said nothin' he ort not to _me_."
10068-8{_}2334{_}The suggestion of Stoddard's making advances of unseemly warmth to Mandy Meacham produced a subdued snicker.
10068-8{_}2335{_}Even Pap smiled, and Mandy herself, who had been looking a bit terrified after her bold speaking, was reassured.
10068-8{_}2336{_}Buckheath had been a week at the Himes boarding-house, finding it not unpleasant to show Johnnie Consadine how many of the girls regarded him with favour, whether she did or not, when he came to supper one evening with a gleam in his eye that spoke evil for some one.
10068-8{_}2337{_}After the meal was over, he followed Pap out on the porch and sat down beside the old man, the girls being bunched expectantly on the step, for he was apt to delay for a bit of chat with one or another of them before leaving.
10068-8{_}2338{_}"You infernal old rascal, I've caught up with you," he whispered, leaning close to his host.
10068-8{_}2339{_}Himes clutched the pipe in his teeth till it clicked, and stared in helpless resentment at his mealer.
10068-8{_}2340{_}"What's the matter with you?"
10068-8{_}2341{_}he demanded.
10068-8{_}2342{_}"Speak lower, so the gals won't hear you, or you'll wish you had," counselled Shade.
10068-8{_}2343{_}"I sent that there thing on to Washington to get a patent on it, and now I find that they was a model of the same there in the name of Gideon Himes.
10068-8{_}2344{_}What do you make of that?"
10068-8{_}2345{_}Pap stared at the thin strips of metal lying in Shade's hard, brown palm.
10068-8{_}2346{_}"The little liar!"
10068-8{_}2347{_}he breathed.
10068-8{_}2348{_}"She told me she got it up herself."
10068-8{_}2349{_}He glared at the bits of steel with protruding eyes, and breathed hard.
10068-8{_}2350{_}"Well, she didn't," Shade countered swiftly, taking advantage of the turn things were showing.
10068-8{_}2351{_}"I made six of 'em; and when I told her to bring 'em back and I'd give her some that would wear better, she only brought me five.
10068-8{_}2352{_}She said she'd lost one here at home, she believed.
10068-8{_}2353{_}I might have knowed then that you'd get your claws on it ef I wasn't mighty peart."
10068-8{_}2354{_}Old Gideon was not listening; he had fallen into a brown study, turning the piece of metal in his skilful, wonted, knotty fingers, with their spade tips.
10068-8{_}2355{_}"Put it out of sight--quick--here she comes!"
10068-8{_}2356{_}whispered Shade; and the old man looked up to see Johnnie Consadine in the doorway.
10068-8{_}2357{_}A grin of triumph grew slowly upon his face, as he gazed from one to the other.
10068-8{_}2358{_}"She did get it up!"
10068-8{_}2359{_}he returned in Buckheath's face.
10068-8{_}2360{_}"You liar!
10068-8{_}2361{_}You're a-aimin' to steal it from her.
10068-8{_}2362{_}You filed out the pieces like she told you to, and when you found it would work, you tried to get a patent on it for yo'se'f.
10068-8{_}2363{_}Yes, sir, I'm onto _you!_"  Shade looked over his shoulder.
10068-8{_}2364{_}The girls had forsaken the steps.
10068-8{_}2365{_}Despairing of his coming, they were strolling two-and-two after Johnnie on the sidewalk.
10068-8{_}2366{_}"It's you and me for it, Pap," he said hardily.
10068-8{_}2367{_}"What was _you_ tryin' to do?
10068-8{_}2368{_}Was you gettin' the patent for Johnnie?
10068-8{_}2369{_}Shall I call her up here and ask her?"
10068-8{_}2370{_}"No, no," exclaimed the old man hastily.
10068-8{_}2371{_}"They ain't no use of puttin' sich things in a fool gal's hands.
10068-8{_}2372{_}She never heard of a patent--wouldn't know one from a hole in the ground.
10068-8{_}2373{_}Hit's like you say, Buck--you and me for it."
10068-8{_}2374{_}The two men rose and stood a moment, Shade smiling a bit to think what he would do with Pap Himes and his claim if he could only once get Johnnie to say yes to his suit.
10068-8{_}2375{_}The thick wits of the elder man apparently realized this feature of the matter not at all.
10068-8{_}2376{_}"Why that thar girl is crazy to get married," he argued, half angrily.
10068-8{_}2377{_}"You know in reason she is--they all are.
10068-8{_}2378{_}The fust night when you brung her here I named it to her that she was pretty well along in years, and she'd better be spry about gettin' her hooks on a man, or she was left.
10068-8{_}2379{_}She said she'd do the best she could--I never heered a gal speak up pearter--most of 'em would be 'shamed to name it out so free.
10068-8{_}2380{_}Why, if it was me, I'd walk her down to a justice's office an' wed her so quick her head'd swim.
10068-8{_}2381{_}"Who's that talking about getting married?"
10068-8{_}2382{_}called Johnnie's voice from the street, and Johnnie herself ran up the steps.
10068-8{_}2383{_}"Hit was me," harangued Pap Himes doggedly.
10068-8{_}2384{_}"I was tellin' Shade how bad you wanted to git off, and that I 'lowed you'd be a good bargain for him."
10068-8{_}2385{_}He looked hopefully from one to the other, as though he expected to see his advice accepted and put into immediate practice.
10068-8{_}2386{_}Johnnie laughed whole-heartedly.
10068-8{_}2387{_}"Pap," she said with shining eyes, "if you get me a husband, I'll have to give you a commission on it.
10068-8{_}2388{_}Looks like I can't noways get one for myself, don't it?"
10068-8{_}2389{_}She passed into the house, and Shade regarded his ally in helpless anger.
10068-8{_}2390{_}"That's the way she talks, here lately," he growled, "Seems like it would be easy enough to come to something; and by the Lord, it would, with any other gal I ever seed--or with Johnnie like she was when she first came down here!
10068-8{_}2391{_}But these days and times she's got a way of puttin' me off that I can't seem to get around."
10068-8{_}2392{_}Neither man quite understood the power of that mental culture which Johnnie was assimilating so avidly.
10068-8{_}2393{_}That reading things in a book should enable her--a child, a girl, a helpless woman--to negative their wishes smilingly, this would have been a thing quite outside the comprehension of either.
10068-8{_}2394{_}"Aunt Mavity wants me to go down to the store for her," Johnnie announced, returning.
10068-8{_}2395{_}"Any of you girls like to come along?"
10068-8{_}2396{_}Mandy had parted her lips to accept the general invitation, when Shade Buckheath rose to his feet and announced curtly, "I'll go with you."
10068-8{_}2397{_}His glance added that nobody else was wanted, and Mandy subsided into a seat on the steps and watched the two walk away side by side.
10068-8{_}2398{_}"Looks like you ain't just so awful pleased to have me boardin' with Pap," Shade began truculently, when it appeared that the girl was not going to open any conversation with him.
10068-8{_}2399{_}"Maybe you wasn't a-carin' for my company down street this evenin'."
10068-8{_}2400{_}"No," said Johnnie, bluntly but very quietly.
10068-8{_}2401{_}"I wish you hadn't come to the house to board.
10068-8{_}2402{_}I have told you to let me alone."
10068-8{_}2403{_}Shade laughed, an exasperated, mirthless laugh.
10068-8{_}2404{_}"You know well enough what made me do it," he said sullenly.
10068-8{_}2405{_}"If you don't want me to board with Pap Himes you can stop it any day you say the word.
10068-8{_}2406{_}You promise to wed me, and I'll go back to the Inn.
10068-8{_}2407{_}The Lord knows they feed you better thar, and I believe in my soul the gals at Pap Himes's will run me crazy.
10068-8{_}2408{_}But as long as you hang off the way you do about our marryin', and I git word of you carryin' on with other folks, I'm goin' to stay where I can watch you."
10068-8{_}2409{_}"Other folks!"
10068-8{_}2410{_}echoed Johnnie, colour coming into her cheeks.
10068-8{_}2411{_}"Shade, there's no use of your quarrelling with me, and I see it's what you're settin' out to do."
10068-8{_}2412{_}"Yes, other folks--Mr. Gray Stoddard, for instance.
10068-8{_}2413{_}I ain't got no auto to take you out ridin' in, but you're a blame sight safer with me than you are with him; and if I was to carry word to your mother or your uncle Pros about your doin's they'd say--"  "The last word my uncle Pros left with ma to give me was that you'd bear watchin', Shade Buckheath," laughed Johnnie, her face breaking up into sweet, sudden mirth at the folly of it all.
10068-8{_}2414{_}"You're not aimin' for my good.
10068-8{_}2415{_}I don't see what on earth makes you talk like you wanted to marry me."
10068-8{_}2416{_}"Because I do," said Buckheath helplessly.
10068-8{_}2417{_}He wondered if the girl did not herself know her own attractions, forgetful that he had not seen them plainly till a man higher placed in the social scale set the cachet of a gentleman's admiration upon them.
10068-8{_}2418{_}CHAPTER XII  THE CONTENTS OF A BANDANNA  It was a breathless August evening; all day the land had lain humming and quivering beneath the glare of the sun.
10068-8{_}2419{_}It seemed that such heat must culminate in a thunder shower.
10068-8{_}2420{_}Even Pap Himes had sought the coolest corner of the porch, his pipe put out, as adding too much to the general swelter, and the hot, yellow cat perched at a discreet distance.
10068-8{_}2421{_}The old man's dreamy eyes were fixed with a sort of animal content on the winding road that disappeared in the rise of the gap.
10068-8{_}2422{_}If was his boast that God Almighty never made a day too hot for him, and to the marrow of them his rheumatic bones felt and savoured the comfort of this blistering weather.
10068-8{_}2423{_}High up on the road he had noted a small moving speck that appeared and disappeared as the foliage hid it, or gaps in the trees revealed it.
10068-8{_}2424{_}It was not yet time for the mill operatives to be out; but as he glanced eagerly in the direction of the buildings, the gates opened and the loom-fixers streamed forth.
10068-8{_}2425{_}Pap had matters of some importance to discuss with Shade Buckheath, and he was glad to see the young man's figure come swinging down the street.
10068-8{_}2426{_}The two were soon deep in a whispered discussion, their heads bent close together.
10068-8{_}2427{_}The little speck far up the road between the trees announced itself to the eye now as a moving figure, walking down toward Cottonville.
10068-8{_}2428{_}"Well, I'll read it again, if you don't believe me," Buckheath said impatiently.
10068-8{_}2429{_}"All that Alabama mill wants is to have me go over there and put this trick on their jennies, and if it works they'll give us a royalty of--well, I'll make the bargain."
10068-8{_}2430{_}"Or I will," countered Pap swiftly.
10068-8{_}2431{_}"You?"
10068-8{_}2432{_}inquired Shade contemptuously.
10068-8{_}2433{_}"Time they wrote some of the business down and you couldn't read it, whar'd you be, and whar'd our money be?"
10068-8{_}2434{_}The moving speck on the road appeared at this time to be the figure of a tall man, walking unsteadily, reeling from side to side of the road, yet approaching the village.
10068-8{_}2435{_}"Shade," pacified Himes, with a truckling manner that the younger man's aggressions were apt to call out in him, "you know I don't mean anything against you, but I believe in my soul I'd ruther sell out the patent.
10068-8{_}2436{_}That man in Lowell said he'd give twenty thousand dollars if it was proved to work--now didn't he?"
10068-8{_}2437{_}"Yes, and by the time it's proved to work we'll have made three times that much out of it.
10068-8{_}2438{_}There ain't a spinning mill in the country that won't save money by putting in the indicator, and paying us a good royalty on it.
10068-8{_}2439{_}If Johnnie and me was wedded, I'd go to work to-morrow advertising the thing."
10068-8{_}2440{_}"The gal ain't in the mill this afternoon, is she?"
10068-8{_}2441{_}asked old Himes.
10068-8{_}2442{_}"No, she's gone off somewheres with some folks Hardwick's sister-in-law has got here.
10068-8{_}2443{_}If you want to find her these days, you've got to hunt in some of the swell houses round on the hills."
10068-8{_}2444{_}He spoke with bitterness, and Pap nodded comprehendingly; the subject was an old one between them.
10068-8{_}2445{_}Then Shade drew from his pocket a letter and prepared to read it once more to the older man.
10068-8{_}2446{_}"Whar's Johnnie?"
10068-8{_}2447{_}Himes started so violently that he disturbed the equilibrium of his chair and brought the front legs to the floor with a slam, so that he sat staring straight ahead.
10068-8{_}2448{_}Shade Buckheath whirled and saw Pros Passmore standing at the foot of the steps--the moving speck come to full size.
10068-8{_}2449{_}The old man was a wilder-looking figure than usual.
10068-8{_}2450{_}He had no hat on, and a bloody cloth bound around his head confined the straggling gray locks quaintly.
10068-8{_}2451{_}The face was ghastly, the clothing in tatters, and his hands trembled as they clutched a bandanna evidently full of some small articles that rattled together in his shaking grasp.
10068-8{_}2452{_}"Good Lord--Pros!
10068-8{_}2453{_}You mighty nigh scared me out of a year's growth," grumbled Pap, hitching vainly to throw his chair back into position.
10068-8{_}2454{_}"Come in.
10068-8{_}2455{_}Come in.
10068-8{_}2456{_}You look like you'd been seein' trouble."
10068-8{_}2457{_}"Whar's Johnnie?"
10068-8{_}2458{_}repeated old Pros hollowly.
10068-8{_}2459{_}It was the younger man who answered this time, with an ugly lift of the lip over his teeth, between a sneer and a snarl.
10068-8{_}2460{_}"She's gone gaddin' around with some of her swell friends.
10068-8{_}2461{_}She may be home before midnight, an' then again she may not," he said.
10068-8{_}2462{_}The old man collapsed on the lower step.
10068-8{_}2463{_}"I wish't Johnnie was here," he said querulously.
10068-8{_}2464{_}"I--" he looked about him confusedly--"I've found her silver mine."
10068-8{_}2465{_}At the words the two on the porch became suddenly rigid.
10068-8{_}2466{_}Then Buckheath sprang down the steps, caught Passmore under the arm-pits and half led, half dragged him up to a chair, into which he thrust him with little ceremony.
10068-8{_}2467{_}He stood before the limp figure, peering into the newcomer's face with eyes of greed and hands that clenched and unclenched themselves automatically.
10068-8{_}2468{_}"You've found the silver mine!"
10068-8{_}2469{_}he volleyed excitedly.
10068-8{_}2470{_}"Whose land is it on?
10068-8{_}2471{_}Have you got options yet?
10068-8{_}2472{_}My grandpappy always said they was a silver mine--"  "Hush!"
10068-8{_}2473{_}Pap Himes's voice hissed across the loud explosive tones.
10068-8{_}2474{_}"No need to tell your business to the town.
10068-8{_}2475{_}I'll bet Pros ain't thought about no options yit.
10068-8{_}2476{_}He may need friends to he'p him out on such matters; and here's you and me, Buck--God knows he couldn't have better ones."
10068-8{_}2477{_}The old man stared about him in a dazed fashion.
10068-8{_}2478{_}"I've got my specimens in this here bandanner," he explained quaveringly.
10068-8{_}2479{_}"I fell over the ledge, was the way I chanced upon it at the last, and I lay dead for a spell.
10068-8{_}2480{_}My head's busted right bad.
10068-8{_}2481{_}But the ore specimens, they're right here in the bandanner, and I aimed to give 'em to Johnnie--to put 'em right in her lap--the best gal that ever was--and say to her, 'Here's your silver mine, honey, that your good-for-nothin' old uncle found for ye; now you can live like a lady!'
10068-8{_}2482{_}That's what I aimed to say to Johnnie.
10068-8{_}2483{_}I didn't aim that nobody else should tetch them samples till she'd saw 'em."
10068-8{_}2484{_}Himes and Buckheath were exchanging glances across the old man's bent, gray head.
10068-8{_}2485{_}Common humanity would have suggested that they offer him rest or refreshment, but these two were intent only on what the bandanna held.
10068-8{_}2486{_}What is it in the thought of wealth from the ground that so intoxicates, so ravishes away from all reasonable judgment, the generality of mankind?
10068-8{_}2487{_}People never seem to conceive that there might be no more than moderate repayal for great toil in a mine of any sort.
10068-8{_}2488{_}The very word mine suggests to them tapping the vast treasure-house of the world, and drawing an unlimited share--wealth lavish, prodigal, intemperate.
10068-8{_}2489{_}These two were as mad with greed at the thought of the silver mine in the mountains as ever were forty-niners in the golden days of California, or those more recent ignoble martyrs who strewed their bones along the icy trails of the Klondike.
10068-8{_}2490{_}"Ye better let me look at 'em Pros," wheedled Pap Himes.
10068-8{_}2491{_}"I know a heap about silver ore.
10068-8{_}2492{_}I've worked in the Georgia gold mines--and you know you never find gold without silver.
10068-8{_}2493{_}I was three months in the mountains with a feller that was huntin' nickel; he l'arned me a heap."
10068-8{_}2494{_}The old man turned his disappointed gaze from one face to the other.
10068-8{_}2495{_}"I wish't Johnnie was here," he repeated his plaintive formula, as he raised the handkerchief and untied the corners.
10068-8{_}2496{_}Pap glanced apprehensively up and down the street; Buckheath ran to the door and shut it, that none in the house might see or overhear; and then the three stared at the unpromising-looking, earthy bits of mineral in silence.
10068-8{_}2497{_}Finally Himes put down a stubby forefinger and stirred them meaninglessly.
10068-8{_}2498{_}"Le' me try one with my knife," he whispered, as though there were any one to hear him.
10068-8{_}2499{_}"All right," returned the old man nervelessly.
10068-8{_}2500{_}"But hit ain't soft enough for lead--if that's what you're meanin'.
10068-8{_}2501{_}I know that much.
10068-8{_}2502{_}A lead mine is a mighty good thing.
10068-8{_}2503{_}Worth as much as silver maybe; but this ain't lead."
10068-8{_}2504{_}A curious tremor had come over Pap Himes's face as he furtively compared the lump of ore he held in his hand with something which he took from his pocket.
10068-8{_}2505{_}He seemed to come to some sudden resolution.
10068-8{_}2506{_}"No, 'tain't lead--and 'tain't nothin'," he declared contemptuously, flinging the bit he held back into the handkerchief.
10068-8{_}2507{_}"Pros Passmore--ye old fool--you come down here and work us all up over some truck that wasn't worth turnin' with a spade!
10068-8{_}2508{_}You might as well throw them things away.
10068-8{_}2509{_}Whar in the nation did you git 'em, anyhow?"
10068-8{_}2510{_}Passmore stumbled to his feet.
10068-8{_}2511{_}He had eaten nothing for three days.
10068-8{_}2512{_}The fall over the ledge had injured him severely.
10068-8{_}2513{_}He was scarcely sane at the moment.
10068-8{_}2514{_}"Ain't they no 'count?"
10068-8{_}2515{_}he asked pitifully.
10068-8{_}2516{_}"Why, I made shore they was silver.

10068-8{_}2518{_}"Leave 'em here, Pros, and go in.

10068-8{_}2520{_}The bandanna slipped rattling from the old man's relaxed fingers.
10068-8{_}2521{_}The specimens clattered and rolled on the porch floor.
10068-8{_}2522{_}With drooping head he shambled through the door.
10068-8{_}2523{_}A woman's face disappeared for a moment from the shadowy front-room window, only to reappear and watch unseen.
10068-8{_}2524{_}Mavity was listening in a sort of horror as she heard her father's tones.
10068-8{_}2525{_}"Git down and pick 'em up--every one!

10068-8{_}2527{_}Yo' eyes is younger'n mine.

10068-8{_}2530{_}"What is it?"

10068-8{_}2532{_}"I thort you had some game on hand."

10068-8{_}2534{_}"Air they really silver?"
10068-8{_}2535{_}[Illustration: HE LOOMED ABOVE THEM, WHITE AND SHAKING.
10068-8{_}2536{_}"YOU THIEVES," HE ROARED.
10068-8{_}2537{_}"GIVE ME MY BANDANNER!

10068-8{_}2539{_}"No--better'n that.
10068-8{_}2540{_}They're nickel.
10068-8{_}2541{_}The feller that was here from the North said by the dips and turns of the stratagems an' such-like we was bound to have nickel in these here mountains somewhar.
10068-8{_}2542{_}A nickel mine's better'n a gold mine--an' these is nickel.
10068-8{_}2543{_}I know 'em by the piece o' nickel ore from the Canady mines that I carry constantly in my pocket.
10068-8{_}2544{_}We'll keep the old fool out of the knowin' of it, and find whar the mine is at, and we'll--"  The two men squatted on the floor, tallying over the specimens they had already collected, and looking about them for more.
10068-8{_}2545{_}In the doorway behind them appeared a face, gaunt, grimed, a blood-stained bandage around the brow, and a pair of glowing, burning eyes looking out beneath.
10068-8{_}2546{_}Uncle Pros had failed to find Mavity Bence, and was returning.
10068-8{_}2547{_}Too dazed to comprehend mere words, the old prospector read instantly and aright the attitude and expression of the two.
10068-8{_}2548{_}As they tied the last knot in the handkerchief, he loomed above them, white and shaking.
10068-8{_}2549{_}"You thieves!"
10068-8{_}2550{_}he roared.
10068-8{_}2551{_}"Give me my bandanner!
10068-8{_}2552{_}Give me Johnnie's silver mine!"
10068-8{_}2553{_}"Yes--yes--yes!
10068-8{_}2554{_}Don't holler it out that-a-way!"
10068-8{_}2555{_}whispered Pap Himes from the floor, where he crouched, still clutching the precious bits of ore.  "We was a-goin' to give 'em to you, Uncle Pros.
10068-8{_}2556{_}We was just foolin'," Buckheath attempted to reassure him.
10068-8{_}2557{_}The old man bent forward and shot down a long arm to recover his own.
10068-8{_}2558{_}He missed the bandanna, and the impetus of the movement sent him staggering a pace or two forward.
10068-8{_}2559{_}At the porch edge he strove to recover himself, failed, and with a short, coughing groan, pitched down the steps and lay, an inert mass, at their foot.
10068-8{_}2560{_}"Cover that handkecher up," whispered Himes before either man moved to his assistance.
10068-8{_}2561{_}CHAPTER XIII  A PATIENT FOR THE HOSPITAL  When the Hardwick carriage drove up in the heavy, ill-odoured August night, and stopped at the gate to let Johnnie Consadine out, Pap Himes's boarding-house was blazing with light from window and doorway, clacking and humming like a mill with the sound of noisy footsteps and voices.
10068-8{_}2562{_}Three or four men argued and talked loudly on the porch.
10068-8{_}2563{_}Through the open windows of the front room, Johnnie had a glimpse of a long, stark figure lying on the lounge, and a white face which struck her with a strange pang of vague yet alarming resemblance.
10068-8{_}2564{_}She made her hasty thanks to Miss Sessions and hurried in.
10068-8{_}2565{_}Gray Stoddard's horse was standing at the hitching post in front, and Gray met her at the head of the steps.
10068-8{_}2566{_}Stoddard looked particularly himself in riding dress.
10068-8{_}2567{_}Its more unconventional lines suited him well; the dust-brown Norfolk, the leathern puttees, gave an adventurous turn to the expression of a personality which was only so on the mental side.
10068-8{_}2568{_}He always rode bareheaded, and the brown hair, which he wore a little longer than other men's, was tossed from its masculine primness to certain hyacinthine lines which were becoming.
10068-8{_}2569{_}Just now his clear brown eyes were luminous with feeling.
10068-8{_}2570{_}He put out a swift, detaining hand and caught hers, laying sympathetic fingers over the clasp and retaining it as he spoke.
10068-8{_}2571{_}"I'm so relieved that you've come at last," he said.
10068-8{_}2572{_}"We need somebody of intelligence here.
10068-8{_}2573{_}I just happened to come past a few minutes after the accident.
10068-8{_}2574{_}Don't be frightened; your uncle came down to see you, and got a fall somehow.
10068-8{_}2575{_}He's hurt pretty badly, I'm afraid, and these people are refusing to have him taken to the hospital."
10068-8{_}2576{_}On the one side Himes and Buckheath drew back and regarded this scene with angry derision.
10068-8{_}2577{_}In the carriage below Lydia Sessions, who could hear nothing that was said, stared incredulously, and moved as though to get down and join Johnnie.
10068-8{_}2578{_}"You'll want him sent to the hospital?"
10068-8{_}2579{_}Stoddard urged, half interrogatively.
10068-8{_}2580{_}"Look in there.
10068-8{_}2581{_}Listen to the noise.
10068-8{_}2582{_}This is no fit place for a man with a possible fracture of the skull."
10068-8{_}2583{_}"Yes--oh, yes," agreed Johnnie promptly.
10068-8{_}2584{_}"If I could nurse him myself I'd like to--or help; but of course he's got to go to the hospital, first of everything."
10068-8{_}2585{_}Stoddard motioned the Hardwick driver to wait, and called down to the carriage load, "I want you people to drive round by the hospital and send the ambulance, if you'll be so kind.
10068-8{_}2586{_}There's a man hurt in here."
10068-8{_}2587{_}Lydia Sessions made this an immediate pretext for getting down and coming in.
10068-8{_}2588{_}"Did you say they didn't want to send him to the hospital?"
10068-8{_}2589{_}she inquired sharply and openly, in her tactless fashion, as she crossed the sidewalk.
10068-8{_}2590{_}"That's the worst thing about such people; you provide them with the best, and they don't know enough to appreciate it.
10068-8{_}2591{_}Have they got a doctor, or done anything for the poor man?"
10068-8{_}2592{_}"I sent for Millsaps, here--he knows more about broken bones than anybody in Cottonville," Pap offered sullenly, mopping his brow and shaking his bald head.
10068-8{_}2593{_}"Millsaps is a decent man.
10068-8{_}2594{_}You know what _he's_ a-goin' to do to the sick."
10068-8{_}2595{_}"Is he a doctor?"
10068-8{_}2596{_}asked Stoddard sternly, looking the lank, shuffling individual named.
10068-8{_}2597{_}"He can doctor a cow or a nag better'n anybody ever saw," Pap put forward rather shamefacedly.
10068-8{_}2598{_}"A veterinarian," commented Stoddard.
10068-8{_}2599{_}"Well, they've gone for the ambulance, and the surgeon will soon be here now."
10068-8{_}2600{_}"I don't know nothin' about veterinarians and surgeons," growled Pap, still alternately mopping his bald head and shaking it contemptuously; "but I know that Millsaps ain't a-goin' to box up any dead bodies and send 'em to the medical colleges; and I know he made as pretty a job of doctoring old Spotty has ever I seen.
10068-8{_}2601{_}To be shore the cow died, but he got the medicine down her when it didn't look as if human hands could do it--that's the kind of doctor he is."
10068-8{_}2602{_}"I aim to give Mr. Passmore a teaspoonful of lamp oil--karosene," said the cow doctor, coming forward, evidently feeling that it was time he spoke up himself.
10068-8{_}2603{_}"Lamp oil is mighty rousin' to them as late like he's doin'.
10068-8{_}2604{_}I've used copperas for such--but takes longer.
10068-8{_}2605{_}Some say a dose of turpentine is better lamp oil--but I 'low both of 'em won't hurt."
10068-8{_}2606{_}Johnnie pushed past them all into the front room where the women were running about, talking lot and exclaiming.
10068-8{_}2607{_}A kerosene lamp without a chimney smoked and flared on the table, filling the room with evil odours.
10068-8{_}2608{_}Pros Passmore's white face thrown up against the lounge cushion was the only quiet, dignified object in sight.
10068-8{_}2609{_}"Mandy," said Johnnie, catching the Meacham woman by the elbow as she passed her bearing a small kerosene can, "you go up to my room and get the good lamp I have there.
10068-8{_}2610{_}Then take this thing away.
10068-8{_}2611{_}Where's Aunt Mavity?"
10068-8{_}2612{_}"I don't know.
10068-8{_}2613{_}She's been carryin' on somethin turrible.
10068-8{_}2614{_}Yes, Johnnie, honey--I'll get the lamp for ye."
10068-8{_}2615{_}When Johnnie turned to her uncle, she found Millsaps bending above him, the small can in his hands, its spout approached to the rigid blue lips of the patient with the unconcern of a man about to fill a lamp.
10068-8{_}2616{_}She sprang forward and caught his arm, bringing the can away with a clatter and splash.
10068-8{_}2617{_}"You mustn't do that," she said authoritatively.
10068-8{_}2618{_}"The doctors will be here in a minute.
10068-8{_}2619{_}You mustn't give him anything, Mr.
10068-8{_}2620{_}Millsaps."

10068-8{_}2622{_}"There is some people that has objections to having their kin-folks cyarved up by student doctors.
10068-8{_}2623{_}Then agin, there is others that has no better use for kin than to let 'em be so treated.

10068-8{_}2625{_}But just as you say--just as you say."

10068-8{_}2628{_}With deft, careful fingers she lifted the wet cloths above the bruised forehead.
10068-8{_}2629{_}The hurt looked old.
10068-8{_}2630{_}No blood was flowing, and she wondered a little.
10068-8{_}2631{_}Catching Shade Buckheath's eye fixed on her from outside the window, she beckoned him in and asked him to tell her exactly how the trouble came about.
10068-8{_}2632{_}Buckheath gave her his own version of the matter, omitting, of course, all mention of the bandanna full of ore which lay now carefully hidden at the bottom of old Gideon Himes's trunk.
10068-8{_}2633{_}"And you say he fell down the steps?"
10068-8{_}2634{_}asked Johnnie.
10068-8{_}2635{_}"Who was with him?
10068-8{_}2636{_}Who saw it?"
10068-8{_}2637{_}"Nobody but me and Pap," Shade answered, trying to give the reply unconcernedly.
10068-8{_}2638{_}"I--I seen it," whispered Mavity Bence, plucking at Johnnie's sleeve.
10068-8{_}2639{_}"I was in the fore room here--and I seen it all."
10068-8{_}2640{_}She spoke defiantly, but her terrified glance barely raised itself to the menacing countenances of the two men on the other side of the lounge, and fell at once.
10068-8{_}2641{_}"I never heard nothin' they was sayin'," she made haste to add.
10068-8{_}2642{_}"But I seen Pros fall, and I run out and helped Pap and Shade fetch him in."
10068-8{_}2643{_}Peculiar as was the attitude of all three, Johnnie felt a certain relief in the implied assurance that there had been no quarrel, that her uncle had not been struck or knocked down the steps.
10068-8{_}2644{_}"Why, Pap," she said kindly, looking across at the old man's perturbed, sweating face, "you surely ain't like these foolish folks round here in Cottonville that think the hospital was started up to get dead bodies for the student doctors to cut to pieces.
10068-8{_}2645{_}You see how bad off Uncle Pros is; you must know he's bound to be better taken care of there in that fine building, and with all those folks that have learned their business to take care of him, than here in this house with only me.
10068-8{_}2646{_}Besides, I couldn't even stay at home from the mill to nurse him.
10068-8{_}2647{_}Somebody's got to earn the money."
10068-8{_}2648{_}"I wouldn't charge you no board, Johnnie," fairly whined Himes.
10068-8{_}2649{_}"I'm willin' to nurse Pros myself, without he'p, night and day.
10068-8{_}2650{_}You speak up mighty fine for that thar hospital.
10068-8{_}2651{_}What about Lura Dawson?
10068-8{_}2652{_}Everybody knows they shipped her body to Cincinnati and sold it.
10068-8{_}2653{_}You ort to be ashamed to put your poor old uncle in such a place."
10068-8{_}2654{_}Johnnie turned puzzled eyes from the rigid face on the lounge--Pros had neither moved nor spoken since they lifted and laid him there--to the old man at the window.
10068-8{_}2655{_}That Pap Himes should be concerned, even slightly, about the welfare of any living being save himself, struck her as wildly improbable.
10068-8{_}2656{_}Then, swiftly, she reproached herself for not being readier to believe good of him.
10068-8{_}2657{_}He and Uncle Pros had been boys together, and she knew her uncle one to deserve affection, though he seldom commanded it.
10068-8{_}2658{_}There was a sound of wheels outside, and Gray Stoddard's voice with that of the doctor's.
10068-8{_}2659{_}Shade and Pap Himes still hovered nervously about the window, staring in and hearkening to all that was said, Mavity Bence had wept till her face was sodden.
10068-8{_}2660{_}She herded the other girls back out of the way, but watched everything with terrified eyes.
10068-8{_}2661{_}"He'll jest about come to hisself befo' he dies," the older conspirator muttered to Shade as the stretcher passed them, and the skilled, white-jacketed attendants laid Pros Passmore in the vehicle without so much as disturbing his breathing.
10068-8{_}2662{_}"He'll jest about come to hisself thar, and them pesky doctors 'll have word about the silver mine.
10068-8{_}2663{_}Well, in this world, them that has, gits, mostly.
10068-8{_}2664{_}Ef Johnnie Consadine had been any manner o' kin to me, I vow I'd 'a' taken a hickory to her when she set up her word agin' mine and let him go out of the house.
10068-8{_}2665{_}The little fool!
10068-8{_}2666{_}she didn't know what she was sendin' away."
10068-8{_}2667{_}And so Pros Passmore was taken to the hospital.
10068-8{_}2668{_}His bandanna full of ore remained buried at the bottom of Gideon Himes's trunk, to be fished up often by the old sinner, fingered and fondled, and laid back in hiding; while the man who had carried it down the mountains to fling it in Johnnie's lap lay with locked lips, and told neither the doctors nor Himes where the silver mine was.
10068-8{_}2669{_}August sweated itself away; September wore on into October in a procession of sun-robed, dust-sandalled days, and still Uncle Pros gave no sign of actual recovery.
10068-8{_}2670{_}Johnnie was working hard in the mill.
10068-8{_}2671{_}Hartley Sessions had become, in his cold, lifeless fashion, very much her friend.
10068-8{_}2672{_}Inert, slow, he had one qualification for his position: he could choose an assistant, or delegate authority with good judgment; and he found in Johnnie Consadine an adjutant so reliable, so apt, and of such ability, that he continually pushed more work upon her, if pay and honours did not always follow in adequate measure.
10068-8{_}2673{_}For a time, much as she disliked to approach Shade with any request, Johnnie continued to urge him whenever they met to finish up the indicators and let her have them back again.
10068-8{_}2674{_}Then Hartley Sessions promoted her to a better position in the weaving department, and other cares drove the matter from her mind.
10068-8{_}2675{_}The condition of Uncle Pros added fearfully to the drains upon her time and thought.
10068-8{_}2676{_}The old man lay in his hospital cot till the great frame had wasted fairly to the big bones, following her movements when she came into the room with strange, questioning, unrecognizing eyes, yet always quieted and soothed by her presence, so that she felt urged to give him every moment she could steal from her work.
10068-8{_}2677{_}The hurts on his head, which were mere scalp wounds, healed over; the surgeon at the hospital was unable to find any indentation or injury to the skull itself which would account for the old man's condition.
10068-8{_}2678{_}They talked for a long time of an operation, and did finally trephine, without result.
10068-8{_}2679{_}They would make an X-ray photograph, they said, when he should be strong enough to stand it, as a means of further investigation.
10068-8{_}2680{_}Meantime his expenses, though made fairly nominal to her, cut into the money which Johnnie could send to her mother, and she was full of anxiety for the helpless little family left without head or protector up in that gash of the wind-grieved mountains on the flank of Big Unaka.
10068-8{_}2681{_}In these days Shade Buckheath vacillated from the suppliant attitude to the threatening.
10068-8{_}2682{_}Johnnie never knew when she met him which would be uppermost; and since he had wearied out her gratitude and liking, she cared little.
10068-8{_}2683{_}One thing surprised and touched her a bit, and that was that Shade used to meet her of an evening when she would be coming from the hospital, and ask eagerly after the welfare of Uncle Pros.
10068-8{_}2684{_}He finally begged her to get him a chance to see the old man, and she did so, but his presence seemed to have such a disturbing effect on the patient that the doctors prohibited further visits.
10068-8{_}2685{_}"Well, I done just like you told me to, and them cussed sawboneses won't let me go back no more," Shade reported to Pap Himes that evening.
10068-8{_}2686{_}"Old Pros just swelled hisself out like a toad and hollered at me time I got in the room.
10068-8{_}2687{_}He's sure crazy all right.
10068-8{_}2688{_}He looks like he couldn't last long, but them that heirs what he has will git the writin' that tells whar the silver mine's at.
10068-8{_}2689{_}Johnnie's liable to find that writin' any day; or he may come to hisself and tell her."
10068-8{_}2690{_}"Well, for God's sake," retorted Pap Himes testily, "why don't you wed the gal and be done with it?
10068-8{_}2691{_}You wed Johnnie Consadine and get that writin', and I'll never tell on you 'bout the old man and such; and you and me'll share the mine."
10068-8{_}2692{_}Shade gave him a black look.
10068-8{_}2693{_}"You're a good talker," he said sententiously.
10068-8{_}2694{_}"If I could _do_ things as easy as you can _tell_ 'em, I'd be president."
10068-8{_}2695{_}"Huh!"
10068-8{_}2696{_}grunted the old man.

10068-8{_}2698{_}If I was your age I'd have her Miz Himes before sundown."
10068-8{_}2699{_}"All right," said Buckheath, "if it's so damn' easy done--this here marryin'--do some of it yourself.
10068-8{_}2700{_}Thar's Laurelly Consadine; she's a widow; and more kin to Pros than Johnnie is.
10068-8{_}2701{_}You go up in the mountains and wed her, and I'll stand by ye in the business."
10068-8{_}2702{_}A slow but ample grin dawned on the old man's round, foolish face.
10068-8{_}2703{_}He looked admiringly at Shade.
10068-8{_}2704{_}"By Gosh!"
10068-8{_}2705{_}he said finally.
10068-8{_}2706{_}"That ain't no bad notion, neither.
10068-8{_}2707{_}'Course I can do it.
10068-8{_}2708{_}They all want to wed. And thar's Laurelly--light-minded fool--ain't got the sense she was born with--up thar without Pros nor Johnnie--I could persuade her to take off her head and play pitch-ball with it--Lord, yes!"
10068-8{_}2709{_}"Well, you've bragged about enough," put in Buckheath grimly.
10068-8{_}2710{_}"You git down in the collar and pull."
10068-8{_}2711{_}The old man gave him no heed.
10068-8{_}2712{_}He was still grinning fatuously.
10068-8{_}2713{_}"It 'minds me of Zack Shalliday, and the way he got wedded," came the unctuous chuckle.
10068-8{_}2714{_}"Zack was a man 'bout my age, and his daughter was a-keepin' house for him.
10068-8{_}2715{_}She was a fine hand to work; the best butter maker on the Unakas; Zack always traded his butter for a extry price.
10068-8{_}2716{_}But old as Sis Shalliday was--she must 'a' been all of twenty-seven --along comes a man that takes a notion to her.
10068-8{_}2717{_}She named it to Zack.
10068-8{_}2718{_}'All right,' says he, 'you give me to-morrow to hunt me up one that's as good a butter maker as you air, and I've got no objections.'
10068-8{_}2719{_}Then he took hisself down to Preacher Blaylock, knowin' in reason that preachers was always hungry for weddin' fees, and would hustle round to make one.
10068-8{_}2720{_}He offered the preacher a dollar to give him a list of names of single women that was good butter makers.
10068-8{_}2721{_}Blaylock done so.
10068-8{_}2722{_}He'd say, 'Now this 'n's right fine-looking, but I ain't never tasted her butter.
10068-8{_}2723{_}Here's one that ain't much to look at, but her butter is prime--jest like your gal's; hit allers brings a leetle extry at the store.
10068-8{_}2724{_}This 'n's fat, yet I can speak well of her workin' qualifications,' He named 'em all out to Zack, and Zack had his say for each one.
10068-8{_}2725{_}'The fat ones is easy keepers,' he says for the last one, 'and looks don't cut much figger in this business--it all depends on which one makes the best butter anyhow.'
10068-8{_}2726{_}"Well, he took that thar string o' names, and he left.
10068-8{_}2727{_}'Long about sundown, here he is back and hollerin' at the fence.
10068-8{_}2728{_}'Come out here, preacher--I've got her,' He had a woman in his buggy that Blaylock had never put eyes on in all his born days.
10068-8{_}2729{_}'Wouldn't none o' them I sent ye to have ye?'
10068-8{_}2730{_}the preacher asked Zack in a kind of whisper, when he looked at that thar snaggle-toothed, cross-eyed somebody that Shalliday'd fetched back.
10068-8{_}2731{_}'I reckon they would,' says Zack.
10068-8{_}2732{_}'I reckon any or all of 'em would 'a' had me,' he says.
10068-8{_}2733{_}'I had only named it to three o' the four, and I hadn't closed up with none o' them, becaze I wasn't quite satisfied in my mind about the butter makin'.
10068-8{_}2734{_}And as I was goin' along the road toward the last name you give me, I come up with this here woman.
10068-8{_}2735{_}She was packin' truck down to the store for to trade it.
10068-8{_}2736{_}I offered her a lift and she rid with me a spell.
10068-8{_}2737{_}I chanced to tell her of what I was out after, and she let on that she was a widder, and showed me the butter she had--hit was all made off of one cow, and the calf is three months old.
10068-8{_}2738{_}I wasn't a-goin' to take nobody's word in such a matter, and hauled her on down to the store and seed the storekeeper pay her extry for that thar butter--and here we air.
10068-8{_}2739{_}Tie the knot, preacher; yer dollar is ready for ye, and we must be gittin' along home--it's 'most milkin' time,' The preacher he tied the knot, and Shalliday and the new Miz.
10068-8{_}2740{_}Shalliday they got along home."
10068-8{_}2741{_}The old man chuckled as he had at the beginning of this tale.
10068-8{_}2742{_}"Well, that was business," agreed Shade impatiently.
10068-8{_}2743{_}"When are you goin' to start for Big Unaka?"
10068-8{_}2744{_}The old man rolled his great head between his shoulders.
10068-8{_}2745{_}"Ye-ah," he assented; "business.
10068-8{_}2746{_}But it was bad business for Zack Shalliday.
10068-8{_}2747{_}That thar woman never made a lick of that butter she was a packin' to the settlement to trade for her sister that was one o' them widders the preacher had give him the name of.
10068-8{_}2748{_}Seems Shalliday's woman had jest come in a-visitin' from over on Big Smoky, and she turned out to be the laziest, no-accountest critter on the Unakas.
10068-8{_}2749{_}She didn't know which end of a churn-dasher was made for use.
10068-8{_}2750{_}Aw--law--huh!
10068-8{_}2751{_}Business--there's two kinds of business; but that was a bad business for Zack Shalliday.
10068-8{_}2752{_}I reckon I'll go up on Unaka to-morrow, if Mavity can run the house without me."
10068-8{_}2753{_}CHAPTER XIV  WEDDING BELLS  A vine on Mavity Bence's porch turned to blood crimson.
10068-8{_}2754{_}Its leaves parted from the stem in the gay Autumn wind, and sifted lightly down to join the painted foliage of the two little maples which struggled for existence against an adverse world, crouching beaten and torn at the curb.
10068-8{_}2755{_}In these days Johnnie used to leave the mill in the evening and go directly to the hospital.
10068-8{_}2756{_}Gray Stoddard was her one source of comfort--and terror.
10068-8{_}2757{_}Uncle Pros's injuries brought these two into closer relations than anything had yet done.
10068-8{_}2758{_}So far, Johnnie had conducted her affairs with a judgment and propriety extraordinary, clinging as it were to the skirts of Lydia Sessions, keeping that not unwilling lady between her and Stoddard always.
10068-8{_}2759{_}But the injured man took a great fancy to Gray.
10068-8{_}2760{_}Johnnie he had forgotten; Shade and Pap Himes he recognized only by an irritation which made the doctors exclude them from his presence; but something in Stoddard's equable, disciplined personality, appealed to and soothed Uncle Pros when even Johnnie failed.
10068-8{_}2761{_}The old mountaineer had gone back to childhood.
10068-8{_}2762{_}He would lie by the hour murmuring a boy's woods lore to Gray Stoddard, communicating deep secrets of where a bee tree might be found; where, known only to him, there was a deeply hidden spring of pure freestone water, "so cold it'll make yo' teeth chatter"; and which one of old Lead's pups seemed likely to turn out the best coon dog.
10068-8{_}2763{_}When Stoddard's presence and help had been proffered to herself, Johnnie had not failed to find a gracious way of declining or avoiding; but you cannot reprove a sick man--a dying man.
10068-8{_}2764{_}She could not for the life of her find a way to insist that Uncle Pros make less demand on the young mill owner's time.
10068-8{_}2765{_}And so the two of them met often at the bedside, and that trouble which was beginning to make Johnnie's heart like lead grew with the growing love Gray Stoddard commanded.
10068-8{_}2766{_}She told herself mercilessly that it was presumption, folly, wickedness; she was always going to be done with it; but, once more in his presence, her very soul cried out that she was indeed fit at least to love him, if not to hope for his love in turn.
10068-8{_}2767{_}Stoddard himself was touched by the old man's fancy, and showed a devotion and patience that were characteristic.
10068-8{_}2768{_}If she was kept late at the hospital, Mavity put by a bite of cold supper for her, and Mandy always waited to see that she had what she wanted.
10068-8{_}2769{_}On the day after Shade Buckheath and Gideon Himes had come to their agreement, she stopped at the hospital for a briefer stay than usual.
10068-8{_}2770{_}Her uncle was worse, and an opiate had been administered to quiet him, so that she only sat a while at the bedside and finally took her way homeward in a state of utter depression for which she could scarcely account.
10068-8{_}2771{_}It was dusk--almost dark--when she reached the gate, and she noted carelessly a vehicle drawn up before it.
10068-8{_}2772{_}"Johnnie," called her mother's voice from the back of the rickety old wagon as the girl was turning in toward the steps.
10068-8{_}2773{_}"Sis' Johnnie--Sis' Johnnie!"
10068-8{_}2774{_}crowed Deanie; and then she was aware of sober, eleven-year-old Milo climbing down over the wheel and trying to help Lissy, while Pony got in his way and was gravely reproved.
10068-8{_}2775{_}She ran to the wheel and put up ready arms.
10068-8{_}2776{_}"Why, honeys!"
10068-8{_}2777{_}she exclaimed.
10068-8{_}2778{_}"How come you-all never let me know to expect you?
10068-8{_}2779{_}Oh, I'm so glad, mother.
10068-8{_}2780{_}I didn't intend to send you word to come; but I was feeling so blue.
10068-8{_}2781{_}I sure wanted to.
10068-8{_}2782{_}Maybe Uncle Pros might know you--or the baby--and it would do him good."
10068-8{_}2783{_}She had got little Deanie out in her arms now, and stood hugging the child, bending to kiss Melissa, finding a hand to pat Milo's shoulder and rub Pony's tousled poll.
10068-8{_}2784{_}"Oh, I'm so glad!--I'm so glad to see you-all," she kept repeating.
10068-8{_}2785{_}"Who brought you?"
10068-8{_}2786{_}She looked closely at the man on the driver's seat and recognized Gideon Himes.
10068-8{_}2787{_}"Why, Pap!"
10068-8{_}2788{_}she exclaimed.
10068-8{_}2789{_}"I'll never forget you for this.
10068-8{_}2790{_}It was mighty good of you."
10068-8{_}2791{_}The door swung open, letting out a path of light.
10068-8{_}2792{_}"Aunt Mavity!"
10068-8{_}2793{_}cried the girl.
10068-8{_}2794{_}"Mother and the children have come down to see me.
10068-8{_}2795{_}Isn't it fine?"
10068-8{_}2796{_}Mavity Bence made her appearance in the doorway, her faded eyes so reddened with weeping that she looked like a woman in a fever.
10068-8{_}2797{_}She gulped and stared from her father, where in the shine of her upheld lamp he sat blinking and grinning, to Laurella Consadine in a ruffled pink-and-white lawn frock, with a big, rose-wreathed hat on her dark curls, and Johnnie Consadine with the children clinging about her.
10068-8{_}2798{_}"Have ye told her?"
10068-8{_}2799{_}she gasped.
10068-8{_}2800{_}And at the tone Johnnie turned quickly, a sudden chill falling upon her glowing mood.
10068-8{_}2801{_}"What's the matter?"
10068-8{_}2802{_}she asked, startled, clutching the baby tighter to her, and conning over with quick alarm the tow-heads that bobbed and surged about her waist.
10068-8{_}2803{_}"The children are all right--aren't they?"
10068-8{_}2804{_}Milo looked up apprehensively.
10068-8{_}2805{_}He was an old-faced, anxious-looking, little fellow, already beginning to have a stoop to his thin shoulders--the bend of the burden bearer.
10068-8{_}2806{_}"I--I done the best I could, Sis' Johnnie," he hesitated apologetically.
10068-8{_}2807{_}"You wasn't thar, and Unc' Pros was gone, an' I thest worked the farm and took care of mother an' the little 'uns best I knowed how.
10068-8{_}2808{_}But when she--when he--oh, I wish't you and Unc' Pros had been home to-day."
10068-8{_}2809{_}Johnnie, her mind at rest about the children, turned to her mother.
10068-8{_}2810{_}"Was ma sick?"
10068-8{_}2811{_}she asked sympathetically.
10068-8{_}2812{_}Then, noticing for the first time the unwonted gaiety of Laurella's costume, the glowing cheeks and bright eyes, she smiled in relief.
10068-8{_}2813{_}"You don't look sick.
10068-8{_}2814{_}My, but you're fine!
10068-8{_}2815{_}You're as spick and span as a bride."
10068-8{_}2816{_}The old man bent and spat over the wheel, preparatory to speaking, but his daughter took the words from his mouth.
10068-8{_}2817{_}"She is a bride," explained Mavity Bence in a flatted, toneless voice.
10068-8{_}2818{_}"Leastways, Pap said he was a-goin' up on Unaka for to wed her and bring her down--and I know in reason she'd have him."
10068-8{_}2819{_}Johnnie's terror-stricken eyes searched her mother's irresponsible, gypsy face.
10068-8{_}2820{_}"Now, Johnnie," fretted the little woman, "how long air you goin' to keep us standin' here in the road?
10068-8{_}2821{_}Don't you think my frock's pretty?
10068-8{_}2822{_}Do they make em that way down here in the big town?
10068-8{_}2823{_}I bought this lawn at Bledsoe, with the very first money you sent up.
10068-8{_}2824{_}Ain't you a bit glad to see us?"
10068-8{_}2825{_}The lip trembled, the tragic dark brows lifted in their familiar slant.
10068-8{_}2826{_}"Come on in the house," said Johnnie heavily, and she led the way with drooping head.
10068-8{_}2827{_}Called by the unusual disturbance, Mandy left the supper she was putting on the table for Johnnie and ran into the front hall.
10068-8{_}2828{_}Beulah Catlett and one or two of the other girls had crowded behind Mavity Bence's shoulders, and were staring.
10068-8{_}2829{_}Mandy joined them in time to hear the conclusion of Mavity's explanation.
10068-8{_}2830{_}She came through the door and passed the new Mrs. Himes on the porch.
10068-8{_}2831{_}"Why, Johnnie Consadine" she cried.
10068-8{_}2832{_}"Is that there your ma?"
10068-8{_}2833{_}Johnnie nodded.
10068-8{_}2834{_}She was past speech.
10068-8{_}2835{_}"Well, I vow!
10068-8{_}2836{_}I should've took her for your sister, if any kin.
10068-8{_}2837{_}Ain't she pretty?
10068-8{_}2838{_}Beulah--she's Johnnie's ma, and her and Pap has just been wedded."
10068-8{_}2839{_}She turned to follow Johnnie, who was mutely starting the children in to the house.
10068-8{_}2840{_}"Well," she said with a sigh, "some folks gits two, and some folks don't git nary one."
10068-8{_}2841{_}And she brought up the rear of the in-going procession.
10068-8{_}2842{_}"Ain't you goin' to pack your plunder in?"
10068-8{_}2843{_}inquired the bridegroom harshly, almost threateningly, as he pitched out upon the path a number of bundles and boxes.
10068-8{_}2844{_}"I reckon they won't pester it till you git back from puttin' up the nag," returned Laurella carelessly as she swung her light, frilled skirts and tripped across the porch.
10068-8{_}2845{_}"You needn't werry about me," she called down to the old fellow where he sat speechlessly glaring.
10068-8{_}2846{_}"Mavity'll show me whar I can sit, and git me a nice cool drink; and that's all I'll need for one while."
10068-8{_}2847{_}Pap Himes's mouth was open, but no words came.
10068-8{_}2848{_}He finally shut it with that click of the ill-fitting false teeth which was familiar--and terrible--to everybody at the boarding-house, shook out the lines over the old horse, and jogged away into the dusk.
10068-8{_}2849{_}"And this here's the baby," admired Mandy, kneeling in front of little Deanie, when the newcomers halted in the front room.
10068-8{_}2850{_}"Why, Johnnie Consadine!
10068-8{_}2851{_}She don't look like nothin' on earth but a little copy of you.
10068-8{_}2852{_}If she's dispositioned like you, I vow I'll just about love her to death."
10068-8{_}2853{_}Mavity Bence was struggling up the porch steps loaded with the baggage of the newcomers.
10068-8{_}2854{_}"Better leave that for your paw," the bride counselled her.
10068-8{_}2855{_}"It's more suited to a man person to lift them heavy things."
10068-8{_}2856{_}But Mavity had not lived with Pap Himes for nearly forty years without knowing what was suited to him, in distinction, perhaps, from mankind in general.
10068-8{_}2857{_}She made no reply, but continued to bring in the baggage, and Johnnie, after settling her mother in a rocking-chair with the cool drink which the little woman had specified, hurried down to help her.
10068-8{_}2858{_}"Everybody always has been mighty good to me all my life," Laurella Himes was saying to Mandy, Beulah and the others.
10068-8{_}2859{_}"I reckon they always will.
10068-8{_}2860{_}Uncle Pros he just does for me like he was my daddy, and my children always waited on me.
10068-8{_}2861{_}Johnnie's the best gal that ever was, ef she does have some quare notions."
10068-8{_}2862{_}"Ain't she?"
10068-8{_}2863{_}returned Mandy enthusiastically, as Johnnie of the "quare notions" helped Mavity Bence upstairs with the one small trunk belonging to Laurella.
10068-8{_}2864{_}"Look out for that trunk, Johnnie," came her mother's caution, with a girlish ripple of laughter in the tones.
10068-8{_}2865{_}"Hit's a borried one.
10068-8{_}2866{_}Now don't you roach up and git mad.
10068-8{_}2867{_}I had obliged to have a trunk, bein' wedded and comin' down to the settlement this-a-way.
10068-8{_}2868{_}I only borried Mildred Faidley's.
10068-8{_}2869{_}She won't never have any use for it.
10068-8{_}2870{_}Evelyn Toler loaned me the trimmin' o' this hat--ain't it sightly?"
10068-8{_}2871{_}Johnnie's distressed eyes met the pale gaze of Aunt Mavity across the little oilcloth-covered coffer.
10068-8{_}2872{_}"I would 'a' told you, Johnnie," said the poor woman deprecatingly, "but I never knowed it myself till late last night, and I hadn't the heart to name it at breakfast.
10068-8{_}2873{_}I thort I'd git a chance this evenin', but they come sooner'n I was expectin' 'em."
10068-8{_}2874{_}"Never mind, Aunt Mavity," said Johnnie.
10068-8{_}2875{_}"When I get a little used to it I'll be glad to have them all here.
10068-8{_}2876{_}I--I wish Uncle Pros was able to know folks."
10068-8{_}2877{_}The children were fed, Milo, touchingly subdued and apologetic, nestling close to his sister's side and whispering to her how he had tried to get ma to wait and come down to the Settlement, and hungrily begging with his pathetic childish eyes for her to say that this thing which had come upon them was not, after all, the calamity he feared.
10068-8{_}2878{_}Snub-nosed, nine-year-old Pony, whose two front teeth had come in quite too large for his mouth, Pony, with the quick-expanding pupils, and the temperament that would cope ill with disaster, addressed himself gaily to his supper and saw no sorrow anywhere.
10068-8{_}2879{_}Little Melissa was half asleep; and even Deanie, after the first outburst of greeting, nodded in her chair.
10068-8{_}2880{_}"I got ready for 'em," Mavity told Johnnie in an undertone, after her father returned.
10068-8{_}2881{_}"I knowed in reason he'd bring her back with him.
10068-8{_}2882{_}Pap always has his own way, and gits whatever he wants.
10068-8{_}2883{_}I 'lowed you'd take the baby in bed with you, and I put a pallet in your room for Lissy."
10068-8{_}2884{_}Johnnie agreed to this arrangement, almost mechanically.
10068-8{_}2885{_}Is it to be wondered at that her mind was already busy with the barrier this must set between herself and Gray Stoddard?
10068-8{_}2886{_}She had never been ashamed of her origin or her people; but this--this was different.
10068-8{_}2887{_}Next morning she sent word to the mill foreman to put on a substitute, and took the morning that she might go with her mother to the hospital.
10068-8{_}2888{_}Passmore was asleep, and they were not allowed to disturb him; but on the steps they met Gray Stoddard, and he stopped so decidedly to speak to them that Johnnie could not exactly run away, as she felt like doing.
10068-8{_}2889{_}"Your mother!"
10068-8{_}2890{_}echoed Stoddard, when Johnnie had told him who the visitor was.
10068-8{_}2891{_}He glanced from the tall, fair-haired daughter to the lithe little gypsy at her side.
10068-8{_}2892{_}"Why, she looks more like your sister," he said.
10068-8{_}2893{_}Laurella's white teeth flashed at this, and her big, dark eyes glowed.
10068-8{_}2894{_}"Johnnie's such a serious-minded person that she favours older than her years," the mother told him.
10068-8{_}2895{_}"Well, I give her the name of the dead, and they say that makes a body solemn like."
10068-8{_}2896{_}It was very evident that Stoddard desired to detain them in conversation, but Johnnie smilingly, yet with decision, cut the interview short.
10068-8{_}2897{_}"I don't see why you hurried me a-past that-a-way," the little mother said resentfully, when they had gone a few steps.
10068-8{_}2898{_}"I wanted to stay and talk to the gentleman, if you didn't.
10068-8{_}2899{_}I think he's one of the nicest persons I've met since I've been in Cottonville.
10068-8{_}2900{_}Mr. Gray Stoddard--how come you never mentioned him to me Johnnie?"
10068-8{_}2901{_}She turned to find a slow, painful blush rising in her daughter's face.
10068-8{_}2902{_}"I don't know, ma," said Johnnie gently.
10068-8{_}2903{_}"I reckon it was because I didn't seem to have any concern with a rich gentleman such as Mr. Stoddard.
10068-8{_}2904{_}He's got more money than Mr. Hardwick, they say--more than anybody else in Cottonville."
10068-8{_}2905{_}"Has he?"
10068-8{_}2906{_}inquired Laurella vivaciously.
10068-8{_}2907{_}"Well, money or no money, I think he's mighty nice.
10068-8{_}2908{_}Looks like he ain't studying as to whether you got money or not.
10068-8{_}2909{_}And if you was meaning that you didn't think yourself fit to be friends with such, why I'm ashamed of you, Johnnie Consadine.
10068-8{_}2910{_}The Passmores and the Consadines are as good a family as there is on Unaka mountains.
10068-8{_}2911{_}I don't know as I ever met up with anybody that I found was too fine for my company.
10068-8{_}2912{_}And whenever your Uncle Pros gets well and finds his silver mine, we'll have as much money as the best of 'em."
10068-8{_}2913{_}The tears blinded Johnnie so that she could scarcely find her way, and the voice wherewith she would have answered her mother caught in her throat.
10068-8{_}2914{_}She pressed her lips hard together and shook her head, then laughed out, a little sobbing laugh.
10068-8{_}2915{_}"Poor ma--poor little mother!"
10068-8{_}2916{_}she whispered at length.
10068-8{_}2917{_}"You ain't been away from the mountains as I have.
10068-8{_}2918{_}Things are--well, they're a heap different here in the Settlement."
10068-8{_}2919{_}"They're a heap nicer," returned Laurella blithely.
10068-8{_}2920{_}"Well, I'm mighty glad I met that gentleman this morning.
10068-8{_}2921{_}Mr. Himes was talking to me of Shade Buckheath a-yesterday.
10068-8{_}2922{_}He said Shade was wishful to wed you, Johnnie, and wanted me to give the boy my good word.
10068-8{_}2923{_}I told him I wouldn't say anything--and then afterward I was going to.
10068-8{_}2924{_}But since I've seen this gentleman, and know that his likes are friends of your'n, well--I--Johnnie, the Buckheaths are a hard nation of people, and that's the truth.
10068-8{_}2925{_}If you wedded Shade, like as not he'd mistreat you."
10068-8{_}2926{_}"Oh mother--don't!"
10068-8{_}2927{_}pleaded Johnnie, scarlet of face, and not daring to raise her eyes.
10068-8{_}2928{_}"What have I done now?"
10068-8{_}2929{_}demanded Laurella with asperity.
10068-8{_}2930{_}"You mustn't couple my name with Mr. Stoddard's that way," Johnnie told her.
10068-8{_}2931{_}"He's never thought of me, except as a poor girl who needs help mighty bad; and he's so kind-hearted and generous he's ready to do for each and every that's worthy of it.
10068-8{_}2932{_}But--not that way--mother, you mustn't ever suppose for a minute that he'd think of me in that way."
10068-8{_}2933{_}"Well, I wish't I may never!"
10068-8{_}2934{_}Laurella exclaimed.
10068-8{_}2935{_}"Did I mention any particular way that the man was supposed to be thinking about you?
10068-8{_}2936{_}Can't I speak a word without your biting my head off for it?
10068-8{_}2937{_}As for what Mr. Gray Stoddard thinks of you, let me tell you, child, a body has only to see his eyes when he's looking at you."
10068-8{_}2938{_}"Mother--Oh, mother!"
10068-8{_}2939{_}protested Johnnie.
10068-8{_}2940{_}"Well, if he can look that way I reckon I can speak of it," returned Laurella, with some reason.
10068-8{_}2941{_}"I want you to promise never to name it again, even to me," said Johnnie solemnly, as they came to the steps of the big lead-coloured house.
10068-8{_}2942{_}"You surely wouldn't say such a thing to any one else.
10068-8{_}2943{_}I wish you'd forget it yourself."
10068-8{_}2944{_}"We-ell," hesitated Laurella, "if you feel so strong; about it, I reckon I'll do as you say.
10068-8{_}2945{_}But there ain't anything in that to hinder me from being friends with Mr. Stoddard.
10068-8{_}2946{_}I feel sure that him and me would get on together fine.
10068-8{_}2947{_}He favours my people, the Passmores.
10068-8{_}2948{_}My daddy was just such an upstanding, dark-complected feller as he is.
10068-8{_}2949{_}He's got the look in the eye, too."
10068-8{_}2950{_}Johnnie gasped as she remembered that the grandfather of whom her mother spoke was Virgil Passmore, and called to mind the story of the borrowed wedding coat.
10068-8{_}2951{_}CHAPTER XV  THE FEET OF THE CHILDREN  The mountain people, being used only to one class, never find themselves consciously in the society of their superiors.
10068-8{_}2952{_}Johnnie Consadine had been unembarrassed and completely mistress of the situation in the presence of Charlie Conroy, who did not fail after the Uplift dance to make some further effort to meet the "big red-headed girl," as he called her.
10068-8{_}2953{_}She was aware that social overtures from such a person were not to be received by her, and she put them aside quite as though she had been, according to her own opinion, above rather than beneath them.
10068-8{_}2954{_}The lover-like pretensions of Shade Buckheath, a man dangerous, remorseless, as careless of the rights of others as any tiger in the jungle, she regarded with negligent composure.
10068-8{_}2955{_}But Gray Stoddard--ah, there her treacherous heart gave way, and trembled in terror.

10068-8{_}3150{_}And in your case it is necessary and right that the younger members of the family should help.
10068-8{_}3151{_}I think you will find that it will not hurt them."
10068-8{_}3152{_}Individuals who work in cotton mills, and are not adults, are never alluded to as children.
10068-8{_}3153{_}It is an offense to mention them so.
10068-8{_}3154{_}They are always spoken of--even those scarcely more than three feet high--as "young people."
10068-8{_}3155{_}Miss Sessions had smiled upon the piteous little group with a judicious mixture of patronage and mild reproof, and her driver had shaken the lines over the backs of the fat horses preparatory to moving on, when Stoddard's car turned into the street from the corner above.

10068-8{_}3157{_}Junius grinned respectfully, while bay Dick dozed and regarded the approaching car philosophically.
10068-8{_}3158{_}As they stood, they blocked the way, so that Gray was obliged to slow down and finally to stop.

10068-8{_}3160{_}His pained eyes went past Lydia Sessions as though she had been but the painted representation of a woman, to fasten themselves on Johnnie where she stood, her tall, deep-bosomed figure relieved against the shining water, the flaxen-haired child on her breast, the little ones huddled about her.
10068-8{_}3161{_}That Johnnie Consadine should have fallen away all at once from that higher course she had so eagerly chosen and so resolutely maintained, had been to Gray a disappointment whose depth and bitterness somewhat surprised him.
10068-8{_}3162{_}In vain he recalled the fact that all his theories of life were against forcing a culture where none was desired; he went back to it with grief--he had been so sure that Johnnie did love the real things, that hers was a nature which not only wished, but must have, spiritual and mental food.
